data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP) is an attractive candidate for Na<sup>+</sup> batteries (SIBs) and Li<sup>+</sup> batteries (LIBs). However, the real implementation of NFPP has been critically restrained by the inferior intrinsic electronic conductivity. Herein, <i>in situ</i> carbon-coated mesoporous NFPP, obtained via freeze drying and heat treatment, demonstrates highly reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup>. Mechanically, the electronic transmission and structural stabilities of NFPP are significantly enhanced by the graphitized carbon coating layer. Chemically, the porous nanosized structure shortens Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths and increases the contact area between the electrolyte and NFPP, ultimately rendering fast ion diffusion. Greatly, long-lasting cyclability (88.5% capacity retention for over 5000 cycles), decent thermal stability at 60 °C, and impressive electrochemical performances are demonstrated in LIBs. The insertion/extraction mechanisms of NFPP in both SIBs and LIBs are systematically investigated, confirming its small volume expansion and high reversibility. The superior electrochemical performances and the insertion/extraction mechanism investigation confirm the feasibility of utilizing NFPP as a cathode material for Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9099%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJinqiang%2BGao%252C%2BYu%2BMei%252C%2BLianshan%2BNi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D62%26issueNum%3D23%26contentID%3Dacs.inorgchem.3c00948%26title%3DAdvanced%2BNASICON-Type%2BNa4Fe3%2528PO4%25292%2528P2O7%2529%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BNa%252B%252FLi%252B%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0020-1669%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dinocaj%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9110%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="hlFld-Abstract synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><span><p class="articleBody_abstractText"><i>In situ</i> carbon-coated mesoporous NFPP demonstrates highly reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup>. The insertion/extraction mechanisms of NFPP in both SIBs and LIBs are systematically investigated by <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD) and <i>ex situ</i> X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) measurements, confirming its small volume change and high reversibility. The insertion/extraction mechanism investigation suggests the feasibility of utilizing NFPP as a cathode material for Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> batteries, which can be extended to various polyanionic insertion materials.</p></span></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Over the past few decades, the demand for environmentally friendly, low-cost, and reliable energy storage applications has been growing rapidly. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 1 to 6">(1−6)</a> For instance, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been utilized in many fields, including large-scale energy storage systems (ESSs) and electric vehicles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a> However, their further developments might be seriously limited by unsatisfactory cycling stability and unfavorable rate capability, which mainly resulted from the sluggish lithium-ion diffusion, phase instability, and unsatisfactory Li<sup>+</sup> insertion reversibility of cathode materials. Hence, the key issue is achieving breakthroughs in LIB cathode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this respect, various groups of electrode materials for LIBs have been investigated, which mainly include layered transition metal oxides such as LiCoO<sub>2</sub> (LCO), LiMO<sub>2</sub> (NCM, M = Ni, Co, and Mn), spinel-type LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and polyanion compounds such as LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (LFP). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> Unfortunately, many critical issues of layered transition metal oxides and spinel-type LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> need to be further improved, such as relatively fast capacity degradation, high-cost transition metal elements (Ni and Co), and poor thermal stability. Meanwhile, polyanion compounds such as LiFePO<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> have been extensively investigated and proved to be attractive candidates for LIBs. Furthermore, many sodium host materials, such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, are also investigated as cathode materials in Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup> batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 14 to 20">(14−20)</a> Inspired by these previous studies, NFPP is proposed to be utilized as a novel lithium host material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21 to 24">(21−24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">NFPP, as one of the most typical sodium-ion superionic conductor (NASICON) structurally mixed phosphates, has been identified as a prospective electrode material for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, the robust structure and crosslinked diffusion channels might be the key factors for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> The new intermediate phase NaFe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) is formed with three Na<sup>+</sup> extracted from the NFPP structure, which allows Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in the framework. Compared with Na<sup>+</sup> (∼1.02 Å, 6-coordinated), the smaller ionic radius of Li<sup>+</sup> (∼0.76 Å, 6-coordinated) enables higher Li<sup>+</sup> transport mobility in NFPP, thus ensuring that NFPP demonstrates a better rate capability in LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Although NFPP has been confirmed to be a promising cathode for SIBs, NFPP used in LIBs, hybrid Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> batteries, PIBs, and ZIBs has rarely been studied. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> To boost its commercial applications, it is necessary to improve its long-term cyclability and rate capability. Additionally, the mechanism of Li<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation in NFPP has not been well investigated yet.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Rietveld refinement of the NFPP sample. (b) Crystalline structure of NFPP, (c) XANES spectra of NFPP, (d) XPS spectrum of C 1s, (e) Raman spectrum of NFPP samples. (f) SEM image of NFPP; the inset is particle size distribution. (g, h) TEM, (i) HRTEM image, and (j) EDS mappings of the NFPP particle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a carbon <i>in situ</i> coated mesoporous NaLi<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (denoted as NLFPP) was successfully prepared via a solid-state route followed by a topotactical ion exchange process with lithium. Facilitated by the construction of the carbon matrix, the growth of NFPP particles is effectively suppressed, which improves the electrochemical performance of NLFPP due to the enhanced electronic conductivity and shortened diffusion distance. Accordingly, the NLFPP cathode presents remarkable cycling stability and outstanding rate performances. Notably, it demonstrates superior cycling performance at high temperature (60 °C) and 98.2% capacity retention is achieved after cycling 200 times at 2C. Meanwhile, full cells were also assembled utilizing commercial hard carbon (HC) as the counter electrode, confirming the high potential for commercial applications. Furthermore, <i>in situ</i> XRD as well as XANES analyses were conducted to investigate the structural changes and redox reactions during the lithium ion insertion/extraction processes. This rational strategy and in-depth understanding make NLFPP a promising cathode material for lithium host materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p"><i>In situ</i> carbon-coated mesoporous NFPP was successfully prepared utilizing freeze drying and heat treatment. The synchrotron X-ray diffraction (SXRD) results were collected to characterize the phase purity and crystallinity of the NFPP sample. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a displays the Rietveld-refined results with <i>R</i>-factors (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 7.26%). The refined lattice parameters and other structural information of the NFPP compound are summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, which well correspond with those of the typical NASICON-type NFPP with an orthorhombic <i><i>Pn</i></i>21<i><i>a</i></i> space group as reported in the previous studies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref32" aria-label="reference 25 and 32">(25,32)</a> The structure of NFPP is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b. NFPP is built by (Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>) layers and (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> The NASICON structure is beneficial for the insertion/extraction processes of multiple Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup>, accommodating the volume expansion/contraction by rotating and distorting. There are four distinguishable sodium sites (Na1, Na2, Na3, Na4) in the crystal of NFPP, and approximately, three Na<sup>+</sup> ions (Na1, Na3, Na4) are involved in the insertion/extraction processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In addition, the atomic ratio of Na, Fe, and P in the NFPP structure determined by inductively coupled plasma (ICP) is 3.98:2.99:4.01 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">XANES measurement was performed to study the oxidation state of iron. LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (Fe<sup>2+</sup>) and FePO<sub>4</sub> (Fe<sup>3+</sup>) were employed as the references. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c shows that the valence of iron in the NFPP sample is determined to be +2. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis along with the XRD patterns of the products after the TG test are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,b</a>, respectively. It is found that the products after calcination are Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (PDF# 24-0072) and Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (PDF# 45-0319), and the carbon content of the as-prepared NFPP is approximately 6.9 wt %, which is derived from the carbonization of citric acid. In addition, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and Raman spectroscopy were performed to characterize the coated carbon material. The XPS spectrum of C 1s for NFPP is fitted as four peaks, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, and the peaks located at 284.7, 285.9, 286.8, and 288.7 eV are corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup> C, sp<sup>3</sup> C, C–O, and O–C═O, respectively. It is worth noting that the sp<sup>2</sup> C accounts for 58.65% of the whole carbon materials in the NFPP sample, which indicates the high graphitization degree of the carbon layer. Two characteristic and intensive bands of the carbon coating layer located at 1342 and 1579 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be clearly found in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e, which correspond to the disorder-induced D-band and G-band, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 22, 35 and 36">(22,35,36)</a> Moreover, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of the NFPP sample is 1.15:1, further confirming that the coated carbon is partially graphitized. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The graphitized carbon can effectively boost the electron transfer and promote the electrochemical property of the electrode. Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) analysis was conducted to investigate the chemical bonds in NFPP. The PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups can be observed, as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the NFPP sample was well investigated. The particle test reveals that the sizes of the samples are 0.5–1.5 μm and the diameter of the small primary particles is ∼100 nm, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f,g. The nanosized particles are beneficial to shorten the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths, thus improving diffusion dynamics. The thickness of the carbon coating layer on NFPP particles is ∼5 nm, as demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image shows conspicuous lattice spacings of 0.294 nm, agreeing well with the (221) plane of the NFPP compound (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i). The NFPP sample exhibits a sponge-like morphology, which is observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>j. Furthermore, EDS elemental mappings reveal that Na, Fe, P, O, and C are homogeneously distributed in NFPP, agreeing well with the refined powder diffraction results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>j). The surface area of NFPP is evaluated to be 18.87 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. In addition, the pore size distribution curves reveal that the pore diameters of the NFPP sample are about 30 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Such relatively large surface area and mesoporous characteristics reveal that the nature of NFPP can effectively increase the contact area between the electrode and electrolyte.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Performance of NFPP</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of NFPP were investigated in SIBs, where sodium metal was employed as an anode. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) plots for NFPP were tested under a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The NFPP//Na cells were first charged to 4.3 V followed by discharging to 2.0 V, and no obvious discrepancies are detected over the five cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), which indicates the highly reversible redox reactions. In addition, CV curves show two pairs of distinct redox peaks, located at about 2.96/2.86 and 3.25/3.17 V, which agreed well with the equilibrium voltages of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions. Galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) analysis was performed at a low current rate of 0.05 C within 1.7–4.3 V. As demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, the NFPP cathode exhibits a capacity of 110.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to approximately 85.8% of its theoretical capacity. Furthermore, two major voltage platforms at 2.92/2.89 and 3.22/3.20 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) are clearly identified in the GCD curves, agreeing well with the CV plots in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) CV plots at the sweeping rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and (b) GCD curves under a current rate of 0.05 C within 1.7–4.3 V for the NFPP sample. (c) Electrochemical performances and (d) corresponding charge–discharge profiles under increasing current densities (0.1, 0.5, 1, 5, 10, 20 C) of the NFPP cathode. (e) Long-cycle performance at a C rate of 2 C. (f) Scheme of the processes for Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from NFPP during the first charge and Li<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation. (g, h) XPS spectra of Na 1s and Li 1s of NFPP and NLFPP. (i) GCD profiles of NFPP in the Na<sup>+</sup> half-cell and NLFPP in the Li<sup>+</sup> half-cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The rate performances of the NFPP cathode were also studied. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d, the prepared sample presents superior rate capability, and a capacity of 67.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is retained at 20C. Furthermore, the discharge capacity is well retained when the current rate converts back to 0.1C. Such an impressive rate performance might be attributed to the high crystallinity and unique conductive matrix constructed with graphitized carbon materials. Generally, cycling under a small C-rate usually leads to poor cycle stability compared to that at a high C-rate, especially during a multi-ion (three Na<sup>+</sup> per formula) insertion/extraction process and more significant volume change. Surprisingly, the Coulombic efficiency is around 100% over the whole long-lasting cycling process, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e. Moreover, an unprecedented cyclability for the NFPP cathode at 2C is achieved, and a discharge capacity of 80.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is retained after 1000 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention over 92.8%, which indicates the excellent long cyclability. The excellent electrochemical performances confirm its superior structural stability, which might facilitate the insertion/extraction of Li<sup>+</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Preparation of the NLFPP Cathode</h3><div class="NLM_p">It is difficult to directly prepare a highly purified NLFPP sample via a common solid-state method because the product is usually mixed with many impurities, such as LiFePO<sub>4</sub>, LiFeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, and Fe(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> In this work, the NLFPP cathode was obtained via a topotactically electrochemical ion-exchange method in a half-cell comprising a NFPP cathode and lithium metal anode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f, about 3 Na<sup>+</sup> ions are extracted from NFPP after full charging, and the other Na<sup>+</sup> is left as the pillar of the crystal, which can stabilize the structure during cycling. Then, the structure is filled with Li<sup>+</sup> during the following discharge process, and the new Li<sup>+</sup> host material is denoted as NaLi<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NLFPP). The composition of NLFPP was measured by the ICP test, and the ratio of Na:Li is about 1.37:2.63, which indicates that the NLFPP is successfully prepared by this electrochemical ion-exchange method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">The structure of NLFPP was evaluated by XRD. Main peaks of the new material, such as (011), (210), (203), (022), (420), and (612), all agreed well with the previously reported NFPP (PDF# 89-0579), as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> demonstrating that the crystal structure still remains after the Na<sup>+</sup> ions are partially substituted with Li<sup>+</sup>. The morphology of NLFPP disassembled from half-cells was investigated by TEM and EDS. The image of HRTEM reveals that NLFPP is uniformly coated with a carbon film, and the planes of (221) with a lattice spacing of 0.292 nm are clearly detected (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>), which is slightly smaller than that of NFPP. Moreover, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b,c</a> demonstrates that the Na, Fe, P, and O elements are evenly distributed in NLFPP. In order to further investigate the existence of Li, XPS spectra were employed to characterize the Na and Li elements in NFPP and NLFPP electrodes, respectively. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g,h, compared with NFPP, the Na1s peak located at 1070.6 eV of NLFPP is significantly reduced, corresponding to the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. Besides, no Li peak is detected in the XPS spectra of NFPP, while the Li1s peak located at 54.8 eV is clearly observed in NLFPP, confirming that the Li<sup>+</sup> is successfully inserted into the structure. To investigate the electrochemical performance of NFPP and NLFPP, the GCD profiles of the two materials were carried out. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i, the discharge capacity of NLFPP is similar with NFPP, corresponding to 110.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the C-rate of 0.05C within 1.7–4.3 V. Notably, compared with NFPP (∼3.1 V <i>vs</i> Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), the NLFPP cathode exhibits a higher average voltage of ∼3.3 V (<i>vs</i> Li/Li<sup>+</sup>), which is attributed to the higher standard potential of Li | Li<sup>+</sup> (−3.04 V <i>vs</i> standard hydrogen electrode, SHE) compared to Na | Na<sup>+</sup> (−2.71 V <i>vs</i> SHE). Such a high average voltage is beneficial for achieving high energy density.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Mechanism of the NFPP Electrode</h3><div class="NLM_p">The <i>in situ</i> XRD measurement was employed to systematically investigate the structural changes of NFPP during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup>. The contour plot of peak intensities and stacked patterns of NFPP and NLFPP and the corresponding GCD profiles during two consecutive cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6a,b</a>, which are recorded in a 2θ range of 20–38° within 1.7–4.3 V at 0.1C. In the beginning, main diffraction peaks located at 23.9, 25.9, 27.3, 28.8, 30.3, 32.2, 33.7, 33.8, 34.0, and 37.0° of both cathodes are indexed well with the typical lattice planes of (410), (402), (203), (013), (221), (222), (420), (004), and (612) for NFPP, confirming its good crystallinity. Similar peak changes are observed during the charge, and several peaks such as (203), (022), (420), and (612) shift to higher angles, resulting from the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> and shrinkage of the crystal lattice. Notably, the (022) peak shifts to a high degree between 1.7 and 3.0 V in the Li<sup>+</sup> cell, while no changes can be observed in the Na<sup>+</sup> cell. This result is likely related to the notable distortion of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> units, which reduce the distance between (Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>) infinite layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Interestingly, the (013) and (222) peaks at 28.8 and 33.4° shift toward lower 2θ values during the charging process in both Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> cells and suddenly shift to a high degree when the NFPP cathodes are charged to high voltages (3.2 V <i>vs</i> Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, 3.4 V <i>vs</i> Li/Li<sup>+</sup>). This phenomenon was also reported by some previous studies, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 and 34">(30,34)</a> and Yang et al. attribute it to an imperfect one-phase reaction rather than an ideal one-phase reaction. Then, all the peaks shift toward reverse directions and turn back to the original positions when both cathodes are fully discharged, suggesting their highly reversible phase transformation. These results indicate that the robust structure is well preserved after the Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Furthermore, continuous lattice parameter changes are determined by the observed peaks, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c–e, which are attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. The unit cell volume of NLFPP is smaller than that of NFPP at the beginning of the second cycle, indicating that the NFPP volume is slightly reduced as the Na<sup>+</sup> ions are substituted with Li<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the volume and cell parameters such as <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> of both cathodes are gradually reduced during the charging process, which resulted from the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup>. When the cathode in the Na<sup>+</sup> cell is charged to 4.3 V, the volume contraction is determined to be 3.84% of the original volume. This value is smaller than that of most typical iron-based electrodes, such as NaFePO<sub>4</sub> (∼17%), Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (∼11%), and Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (∼7%). The opposite changes occur during the following discharge process, and the volume restores to the initial size when it is fully discharged. Compared with the NFPP cathode, the NLFPP demonstrates an even smaller volume change, which is only 2.51%. Such a negligible volume change might be attributed to the robust NASICON structure and the relatively small ion radius of Li<sup>+</sup>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) <i>In situ</i> XRD pattern along with GCD profiles of the NFPP electrode in the Na<sup>+</sup> half-cell. (b) <i>In situ</i> XRD pattern along with GCD profiles of the NFPP cathode in the Li<sup>+</sup> half-cell during two successive cycles. (c–e) Lattice parameters of (a–c) for NFPP and NLFPP cathodes in discharge/charge processes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, XANES analysis was utilized to investigate the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction of NLFPP from the surface to the bulk. First, the spectra at the Fe <i>L</i>-edge before and after the first charge in the Li<sup>+</sup> half-cell are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. Compared with the pristine NFPP cathode, the peaks of <i>L</i><sub>2-low</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>3-low</sub> of NLFPP are significantly reduced after being fully charged, while <i>L</i><sub>2-high</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>3-high</sub> enhanced, indicating the redox reaction of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>. To further investigate the redox reaction in the bulk, the Fe <i>K</i>-edge of the NFPP cathode before and after the first charge and the corresponding Fourier transform of the EXAFS signals were determined. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b demonstrates that the Fe <i>K</i>-edge spectrum shifts from 7128.5 to 7134.3 eV after the first full charging, indicating the redox reaction of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, which is well consistent with <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c shows that the Fe–O distance is slightly reduced after charge, indicating the volume contraction, agreeing well with the <i>in situ</i> XRD results. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The variation of Fe <i>L</i>-edge spectra for NLFPP at different voltages was explored, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d,e. During charging, both the peak intensities of Fe <i>L</i><sub>2-low</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>3-low</sub> are gradually weakened, while Fe <i>L</i><sub>2-high</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>3-high</sub> strengthened, suggesting the valence state change of Fe from +2 to +3. During the following discharge process, the XANES spectra shift to lower energy positions. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f, the Fe <i>L</i><sub>3</sub> peak shifts rightward during charge and it shifts back to its original state when the cell is fully discharged. Furthermore, the valence state and structural change of Fe during charge–discharge processes were studied by <i>ex situ</i> XANES spectra at the Fe <i>K</i>-edge (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g–i). The Fe <i>K</i>-edge energy exhibits a clear increase during the charge process, indicating the oxidation reaction of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and it is followed by returning to its initial position during discharge. Thus, the charge/discharge processes of NLFPP are successive oxidation/reduction reactions of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, which are systematically determined by XANES analysis at both the Fe <i>L</i>- and <i>K</i>-edge, verifying a highly reversible redox reaction process.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a, b) XANES spectra of NFPP cathode before and after first charge. (c) Corresponding Fourier transform of the EXAFS signals at Fe <i>K</i>-edge. (d–f)Spectra at Fe <i>L</i>-edge of NLFPP cathode and corresponding contour plot with GCD profiles. (g– i) Fe <i>K</i>-edge spectra of NLFPP cathode and corresponding contour plot with voltage profile.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Properties of NLFPP Cathodes</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of the NLFPP cathode were investigated in half-cells assembled with Li metal as an anode. CV for NLFPP was tested at a sweeping rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, four distinct couples of redox peaks can be observed, which are located at 3.43/3.38, 3.30/3.22, 3.20/3.09, and 2.93/2.89 V. The cycling performance of the NLFPP cathode was measured at 2C within 2.0–4.0 V. The GCD plots and corresponding dQ/dV curves for various cycles are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,c. Four pairs of voltage plateaus (3.40/3.40, 3.26/3.26, 3.12/3.20, 2.90/2.98 V) are observed, which agree well with the CV curves. Moreover, no obvious discrepancy is detected over the first 100 cycles, implying the high reversibility. As demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, the capacity of NFLPP at 2 C is 87.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 96.1% capacity retention is achieved after more than 1000 cycles, revealing the excellent cycling stability of the NLFPP cathode as a lithium host material. Furthermore, the cycling performances of NLFPP at various current rates were investigated, and the rate capability and GCD plots are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e,f. The discharge capacities at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20C are 106, 101.5, 90.3, 87.5, 80.4, 71.8, and 65.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. A capacity of 102.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained at 0.1C after 60 cycles, corresponding to 96.8% of the initial capacity at the same current rate. These results indicate the outstanding reversibility and decent rate performance of NLFPP. This decent rate capability can be ascribed to the carbon layer, an electron transfer “high way”, and shortened ion diffusion paths provided by mesoporous nanosized particles. Besides, the NLFPP cathode demonstrates outstanding cycling stability in a wider temperature range. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g, the NLFPP cathode exhibits an initial capacity of 99.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1C at 60 °C, which is slightly higher than that at room temperature (91.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Furthermore, a capacity of 80.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be obtained after 300 cycles at 60 °C, indicating that outstanding cyclability is also obtained even at high temperature. It is proposed that the capacity degradation can be ascribed to the corrosion of the electrolyte in conditions of high temperature. The ultralong cycling stability of NLFPP was evaluated at 20 C. Approximately 75.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is delivered, and a capacity retention of 88.5% is achieved after cycling 5000 times, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h, which is much better than many previously reported Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> host materials. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 17 and 42 to 44">(17,42−44)</a> The results mentioned above confirm that the NLFPP cathode is a promising candidate for EESs.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performances of NLFPP. (a) CV plots at the sweeping rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within 1.7–4.3 V (<i>vs</i> Li/Li<sup>+</sup>). (d) Cycle performance of the NLFPP cathode at 2C and corresponding (b) GCD profiles and (c) d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves at various cycles. (e) Electrochemical performances and (f) corresponding charge–discharge profiles under increasing C-rates (0.1, 0.5, 1, 5, 10, 20C) of NLFPP cathode. (g) Cycling performances of NLFPP cathodes in a wide temperature range. (h) Ultralong cycling performances of the NLFPP cathode at 10C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction behaviors of NFPP and NLFPP cathodes, CV analyses at various scanning rates (0.1–0.7 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) were conducted. Two couples of sharp peaks are observed in the CV curves of NFPP, while three major couples of slopes can be observed in NLFPP (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). This is mainly ascribed to the smaller ion radius of the lithium ion (0.76 Å) compared with that of the sodium ion (1.02 Å). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Furthermore, the redox potential difference (Δ<i>V</i><sub>A1–C1</sub> = 73 mV) of NLFPP at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which is mainly attributed to the redox reactions of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>, is relatively lower than that of NFPP (145 mV). The oxidation potential difference (Δ<i>V</i><sub>A1</sub> = 73 mV) of NLFPP between 0.1 and 0.7 mV s<sup>–1</sup> is smaller than that of NFPP (Δ<i>V</i><sub>A1</sub> = 114 mV). Compared with the NFPP cathode, NLFPP exhibits much more symmetrical redox peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b), indicating the smaller polarization of the electrode and faster ion diffusion dynamics, which agreed well with the results of the rate performances. The <i>b</i> values of both NFPP and NLFPP cathodes are mainly distributed in the range between 0.8 and 0.9, implying that the overall charge/discharge processes of both the electrodes can be controlled by faradaic and surface-controlled processes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The contribution of pseudocapacitances during the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes was evaluated, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d,e. The pseudocapacities are determined to be 26.4 and 27.9% of the whole capacities of NFPP and NLFPP cathodes at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. These results are the reason for the high charge transfer and outstanding cycling performance. Furthermore, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was used, with a continuous charge/discharge time of 10 min at a current of 0.1C, and the relaxation time is 60 min. The single-step GITT experiment around 2.9 V of the NFLPP electrode is summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. The GCD profiles of both cathodes are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c, and the corresponding D<sub>Li</sub>/D<sub>Na</sub> is demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f. Notably, the Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients fluctuate over the continuous charge/discharge processes, which is ascribed to Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion from various sites in the host materials. The D<sub>Na</sub> for the NFPP electrode is mainly distributed within 10<sup>–12</sup>–10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, reflecting a sluggish diffusion mobility. The D<sub>Li</sub> for the NLFPP electrode is mainly distributed within the scope of 10<sup>–11</sup>–10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the average D<sub>Li</sub> (9.95 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is much higher than D<sub>Na</sub> (9.83 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), which implies the improved ion diffusion capability in this material.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) CV plots of NFPP under increasing scan rates. (b) CV plots of NFLPP under increasing scan rates. (d, e) CV plots along with pseudocapacitive contributions for NFPP and NFLPP cathodes. (c) GITT plots of NFPP and NLFPP. (f) Diffusion coefficients for Na<sup>+</sup> in NFPP and Li<sup>+</sup> in NLFPP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analyses were conducted for the NLFPP cathode from 10<sup>5</sup> to 10<sup>–1</sup> Hz. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> presents the Nyquist curves and corresponding simplified equivalent circuit model of the NLFPP cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> In the Nyquist plots, the semicircle and the slope line are related to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) and Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), respectively. In the equivalent circuit model, <i>R</i><sub>S</sub> and CPE are solution resistance and constant-phase elements, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The obtained Nyquist plots with the corresponding <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> values of the NLFPP cathode under continuous cycling are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. By comparing the diameters of the semicircles, the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> of NLFPP values are gradually decreased, probably owing to the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) film on the particle surface as well as increased ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>. Although the EIS spectra were tested after full charge process, the ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the NFLPP structure is slightly increased along with the gradual capacity loss, which might contribute to the improved electronic conductivity. Correspondingly, the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> of NLFPP values gradually reduces as the cycle time increases. Furthermore, the spike’s slope rises as the cycle time increases, manifesting the promoted Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the NLFPP structure. As for this result, it is proposed that the increased ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> leads to a few Li vacancies in the NFLPP structure, which facilitate the fast migration of Li<sup>+</sup> in the bulk cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref30 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref30 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 22, 30, 37 and 38">(22,30,37,38)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Full Cell Performances</h3><div class="NLM_p">The potential of the NLFPP electrode for real applications was explored and a full Li-ion cell was configured by combining the NLFPP cathode with a hard carbon (HC) anode (denoted as NLFPP//HC). The configuration and the working mechanism of NLFPP//HC are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a. The SEM image, XRD analysis, and rate performances of HC used in full cells are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/suppl_file/ic3c00948_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a–c</a>. The HC anode was prelithiated in the Li<sup>+</sup> half-cell at 129 mA g<sup>–1</sup> within 0.01–2.0 V (<i>vs</i> Li<sup>+</sup>/Li). CV plots and GCD profiles of NLFPP and HC in Li<sup>+</sup> half-cells are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b,c, respectively. The NLFPP//HC cell delivers a discharge capacity of 90.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05 C within 1.0–4.0 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d). Meanwhile, the full cell exhibits decent rate capability, and capacities of 88.8, 82.9, 76.9, 66.3, 59.6, and 54.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> are obtained at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 3.0, and 5.0C, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f shows that the full cell could power a LED screen, manifesting its potential in practical applications. Additionally, the cyclability of the full cell was investigated, and a capacity retention of 80.9% is obtained after cycling 200 times at 1C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>g). This cycling stability in the full cell is more inferior compared to that in half-cells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d), which might be attributed to the capacity drop of the hard carbon anode.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/medium/ic3c00948_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Configuration of the NLFPP//HC full cell. (b) CV plots and (c) GCD curves of NLFPP and HC. (d) GCD profile and (e) C-rate capability of the full cell. (f) Display screen powered by the full cell. (g) Cycling performance of the NLFPP//HC full cell at 1C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948/asset/images/large/ic3c00948_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The outstanding electrochemical performances of NLFPP resulted from the unique NASICON structure and promoted interface engineering modification. (a) The NASICON framework possesses 3D ion transport channels, and the small size of Li<sup>+</sup> ensures the fast diffusion kinetics, thus confirming a negligible volume change upon Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction during charge/discharge. (b) The graphitized coating–carbon layer constructs a crosslinked conductive matrix, which not only effectively enhances the charge transfer process between NLFPP particles but also prevents the NLFPP cathode from the corrosion of the electrolyte. (c) The nanosized mesoporous structure enables high contact area as well as short Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The NFPP cathode for Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> batteries has been successfully synthesized via a solid-state and topotactical ion-exchange method. A decent rate capability (67.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20C) and superior cyclability (92.8% capacity retention after cycling 1000 times) are obtained in SIBs. Besides, a high capacity of 110.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05C, ultrastable cyclability (88.5% capacity retention over 5000 cycles at 10C), and remarkable rate performance (65.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20C) are achieved in LIBs. Meanwhile, outstanding cyclability is demonstrated at high temperature (81.5% capacity retention after 300 cycles at 60 °C), which is attractive for safe and stable LIBs. Full Li-ion cells are also fabricated with hard carbon as an anode for real applications. Moreover, the insertion/extraction mechanisms of NFPP in Na<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> batteries are comprehensively investigated by various advanced analyses, which confirm the extremely small volume change (2.51%), high electrochemical reversibility, and fast ion diffusion dynamics during charge/discharge. Given this, the NFPP cathode having a safe and stable structure, promising electrochemical properties, and cost-effective features makes it a high-potential competitor for advanced energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2102904</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.202102904</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BFichtner%26author%3DK.%2BEdstr%25C3%25B6m%26author%3DE.%2BAyerbe%26author%3DM.%2BBerecibar%26author%3DA.%2BBhowmik%26author%3DI.%2BE.%2BCastelli%26author%3DS.%2BClark%26author%3DR.%2BDominko%26author%3DM.%2BErakca%26author%3DA.%2BA.%2BJ.%2BA.%2BE.%2BM.%2BFranco%26title%3DRechargeable%2Bbatteries%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bfuture%25E2%2594%2580the%2Bstate%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bart%2Bfrom%2Ba%2BBATTERY%2B2030%252B%2Bperspective%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.202102904&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.inorgchem.3c00948&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.202102904&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rechargeable Batteries of the Future-The State of the Art from a BATTERY 2030+ Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fichtner, Maximilian; Edstrom, Kristina; Ayerbe, Elixabete; Berecibar, Maitane; Bhowmik, Arghya; Castelli, Ivano E.; Clark, Simon; Dominko, Robert; Erakca, Merve; Franco, Alejandro A.; Grimaud, Alexis; Horstmann, Birger; Latz, Arnulf; Lorrmann, Henning; Meeus, Marcel; Narayan, Rekha; Pammer, Frank; Ruhland, Janna; Stein, Helge; Vegge, Tejs; Weil, Marcel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2102904</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The development of new batteries has historically been achieved through discovery and development cycles based on the intuition of the researcher, followed by exptl. trial and error-often helped along by serendipitous breakthroughs.  Meanwhile, it is evident that new strategies are needed to master the ever-growing complexity in the development of battery systems, and to fast-track the transfer of findings from the lab. into com. viable products.  This gives an overview over the future needs and the current state-of-the art of five research pillars of the European Large-Scale Research Initiative BATTERY 2030+, namely battery interface genome in combination with a materials acceleration platform (BIG-MAP), progress toward the development of self-healing battery materials, and methods for operando, sensing to monitor battery health.  These subjects are complemented by an overview over current and up-coming strategies to optimize manufacturability of batteries and efforts toward development of a circular battery economy through implementation of recyclability aspects in the design of the battery.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/D1EE00691F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mrozik, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rajaeifar, M.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Environmental impacts, pollution sources and pathways of spent lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mrozik, Wojciech; Rajaeifar, Mohammad Ali; Heidrich, Oliver; Christensen, Paul</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6099-6121</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  There is a growing demand for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for elec. transportation and to support the application of renewable energies by auxiliary energy storage systems.  This surge in demand requires a concomitant increase in prodn. and, down the line, leads to large nos. of spent LIBs.  The ever-increasing battery waste needs to be managed accordingly.  Currently, there are no universal or unified stds. for waste disposal of LIBs around the globe.  Each country uses one or a combination of practices such as landfilling, incineration and full or partial recycling depending on the no. of batteries leaving the market, current legislation and infrastructures.  Informal disposal or reprocessing is not a rare activity.  This review records, identifies and categorises the environmental impacts, sources and pollution pathways of spent LIBs.  The drawbacks of the disposal practices are highlighted and the threats assocd. with them are discussed.  The evidence presented here is taken from real-life incidents and it shows that improper or careless processing and disposal of spent batteries leads to contamination of the soil, water and air.  The toxicity of the battery material is a direct threat to organisms on various trophic levels as well as direct threats to human health.  Identified pollution pathways are via leaching, disintegration and degrdn. of the batteries, however violent incidents such as fires and explosions are also significant.  Finally, the paper discusses some of the main knowledge gaps for future assessments.  The current study offers a comprehensive overview of the threats and hazards that need to be managed in order to ensure the design and implementation of safe disposal and processing options for spent LIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202101126"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weiss, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruess, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kasnatscheew, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Levartovsky, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Levy, N.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fast Charging of Lithium-Ion Batteries: A Review of Materials Aspects</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Weiss, Manuel; Ruess, Raffael; Kasnatscheew, Johannes; Levartovsky, Yehonatan; Levy, Natasha Ronith; Minnmann, Philip; Stolz, Lukas; Waldmann, Thomas; Wohlfahrt-Mehrens, Margret; Aurbach, Doron; Winter, Martin; Ein-Eli, Yair; Janek, Juergen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2101126</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fast charging is considered to be a key requirement for widespread economic success of elec. vehicles.  Current lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) offer high energy d. enabling sufficient driving range, but take considerably longer to recharge than traditional vehicles.  Multiple properties of the applied anode, cathode, and electrolyte materials influence the fast-charging ability of a battery cell.  In this review, the physicochem. basics of different material combinations are considered in detail, identifying the transport of lithium inside the electrodes as the crucial rate-limiting steps for fast-charging.  Lithium diffusion within the active materials inherently slows down the charging process and causes high overpotentials.  In addn., concn. polarization by slow lithium-ion transport within the electrolyte phase in the porous electrodes also limits the charging rate.  Both kinetic effects are responsible for lithium plating obsd. on graphite anodes.  Conclusions drawn from potential and concn. profiles within LIB cells are complemented by extensive literature surveys on anode, cathode, and electrolyte materials-including solid-state batteries.  The advantages and disadvantages of typical LIB materials are analyzed, resulting in suggestions for optimum properties on the material and electrode level for fast-charging applications.  Finally, limitations on the cell level are discussed briefly as well.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2021.06.003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, S.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.esci.2022.02.006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crack-free single-crystalline Co-free Ni-rich LiNi<sub>0.95</sub>Mn<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered cathode</span>. <i>eScience</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">116</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">124</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature extraction of individual elements from charged spent LiFePO4 batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Mei-Cen; Zhao, Yun; Kang, Yu-Qiong; Wozny, John; Liang, Zheng; Wang, Jun-Xiong; Zhou, Guang-Min; Li, Bao-Hua; Tavajohi, Naser; Kang, Fei-Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Rare Metals (Beijing, China)</span>

However, recovering individual elements from the spent batteries without generating waste is challenging.  Here, we present a distinctive approach for recycling spent LiFePO4 batteries at room temp., where water is the only leaching agent consumed.  FePO4 and lithium intercalated graphite act as a precursor material for selectively extg. lithium, iron, and phosphorus through charging the LiFePO4 batteries to the delithiated state.  NaOH soln. extd. Fe from FePO4 within 30 min and regenerated without consumption, similar to a catalyst.  Under the optimal leaching conditions (1 mol·L-1 NaOH, 0.5 h, NaOH/Fe molar ratio of 4.5), Fe and P leaching efficiencies achieved 89.1% and 99.2%, resp.  The methodol. reflected in this research reduced the material cost per kg cathode material to a fraction of previously published reports, only occupies 6.13% of previous reports.  In addn., the method improved the battery recycling revenue calcd. by the EverBatt model by 2.31 times and 1.94 times over pyrometallurgical and hydrometallurgical methods.  The proposed method allows for the convenient recovery of the elemental components of spent LiFePO4 batteries.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uncovering the Potential of M1-Site-Activated NASICON Cathodes for Zn-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Pu; Zou, Zheyi; Sun, Xingwei; Wang, Da; Ma, Jun; Kong, Qingyu; Xiao, Dongdong; Gu, Lin; Zhou, Xinhong; Zhao, Jingwen; Dong, Shanmu; He, Bing; Avdeev, Maxim; Shi, Siqi; Cui, Guanglei; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1907526</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a long-standing consciousness that the rhombohedral NASICON-type compds. as promising cathodes for Li+/Na+ batteries should have inactive M1(6b) sites with ion (de)intercalation occurring only in the M2 (18e) sites.  Of particular significance is that M1 sites active for charge/discharge are commonly considered undesirable because the ion diffusion tends to be disrupted by the irregular occupation of channels, which accelerates the deterioration of battery.  However, it is found that the structural stability can be substantially improved by the mixed occupation of Na+/Zn2+ at both M1 and M2 when using NaV2(PO4)3 (NVP) as a cathode for Zn-ion batteries.  The results of at.-scale scanning transmission electron microscopy, anal. of ab initio mol. dynamics simulations, and an accurate bond-valence-based structural model reveal that the improvement is due to the facile migration of Zn2+ in NVP, which is enabled by a concerted Na+/Zn2+ transfer mechanism.  In addn., significant improvement of the electronic cond. and mech. properties is achieved in Zn2+-intercalated ZnNaV2(PO4)3 in comparison with those of Na3V2(PO4)3.  This work not only provides in-depth insight into Zn2+ intercalation and dynamics in NVP unlocked by activating the M1 sites, but also opens a new route toward design of improved NASICON cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201401963"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanoflake-assembled hierarchical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C microflowers: superior Li storage performance and insertion/extraction mechanism</span>. <i>Adv.
